إلى الإجماع إن وجد وإلا فإلى أخبار الآحاد وعند تعارضها إلى أقوال الصحابة، ثم إلى القياس فلم قلتم إنه يصار عند تعارض الآيتين إلى أخبار الآحاد، ثم إلى أقوال الصحابة والقياس؟ والجواب عنه : أن الإجماع مرجح ومقدم على الكل عند معارضته إياها : لأنه لا يكون منسوخاً بكتاب أو سنة ولا يكون باطلاً، فتعين أن يكون الكتاب والسنة ولو كانت متواترة، منسوخة، والإجماع كاشف عن النسخ. فعند تعارض الآيتين أو السنتين ووجود الإجماع يعمل بما وافقه الإجماع ويجعل ناسخاً لما خالفه، فقد ترجح بترجيح قطعي، والكلام فيما لا ترجيح فيه، ولا يمكن هناك الإجماع، وأما السنة المتواترة فمثل الآية في إيجاب العمل والقطعية، فالسنة المتواترة تعارض الآية كما تعارضها آية أخرى ولا تضمحل عندها، فلو أهدر للتعارض يلزم تساقط الكل من الآيتين والسنة ومن ههنا اندفع ما في «التلويح» أن اعتبار خبر الواحد عند تعارض الآيتين إما لأن الخبر مرجح لما وافقه فيرد عليه أن لا ترجيح بكثرة الأدلة، وإما لأن المتعارضين تساقطا، فبقي الخبر سالماً عن المعارضة، فمثله يمكن أن يقال
And here are investigations: First: Resorting to what is below them in evidence is not valid, because a single evidence, just as it conflicts with one, conflicts with two. So a verse conflicting with another verse conflicts with the report agreeing with it, and so on. So if conflict drops both verses, it also drops the report below them. The response that khabar al-wahid, when it has no ruling in the presence of a verse, becomes like a follower and supporter, and thus can serve as a preponderant for one of them, so one acts upon the verse agreeing with the report due to this preponderance, is nothing, because preponderance according to us is not by something that by itself is suitable for establishing authority; the report is in itself an authority if there were no verses, so it does not affect preponderance. And it is stated in al-Badi' that the book is preferred over the report because it is a proof by itself, so understand. And al-Sheikh al-Haddad responded in his commentary on Usul of Imam Fakhr al-Islam that two proofs of the same type, i.e., emanating from one speaker, are not considered in conflict, like a sequential speech whose end contradicts its beginning; just as if a witness testifies to an incident then another contradictory testimony, his statement is disregarded and dropped, but the other's statement is not dropped. So here, two verses are speech of one speaker, Allah Almighty, and the Sunnah is speech of another speaker. So if two verses conflict, they become as if nonexistent, and the Sunnah remains without conflict. And he derived from this that when two verses conflict, one resorts to mutawatir Sunnah, because it is speech of another speaker, and if a verse and mutawatir Sunnah conflict, they do not drop. He explained with a detailed discourse. But this lowly servant does not fully understand it because the Sunnah is only through revelation, since there is no ruling except from Allah. So the Sunnah and the Book both reveal a divine ruling. Conflict only occurs between the rulings, and it is the eternal speech of Allah, and it is speech of one speaker. Also: two speeches emanating from one speaker who is truthful in both, who does not err or forget, are equal in correspondence. And if they emanate from another truthful speaker, he has no superiority over those two contradictory speeches. Analogy to the witness is invalid, because they are not definitively true. So if contradictory speech emanates from him, it raises doubt in his memory or integrity, so doubt arises in the truthfulness, so it is not accepted. But here, there is no room for doubt at all. Rather, the speaker of the Sunnah is definitively truthful, like the speaker of the Book, so they must correspond, and that is the conflict. As for the two derived points, they are the fruit of a corrupt tree and a deficiency of the truth in it. The upshot of what can be said in this regard is: when conflict occurs and preference is impossible, then either each of the verse, the report agreeing with it, and the qiyas agreeing with it are all disabled by the other verse's opposition, making action in the incident impossible, and this cannot be. Nor can it be said: act upon the principle, because if the principle is a proof, it is also opposed and thus disabled from authority; if it is not a proof, then action without proof is necessary. Or to act upon one of them by choice, which is a choice between what is forbidden to act upon and what is obligatory to act upon, because one is definitely abrogated, which is forbidden to act upon. Or to act upon one of the two verses without the other, which is preponderance without a preponderant. Or to consider them as two conflicting proofs or not, and not consider what is lower than them. For the weak is destroyed in the face of the strong and cannot oppose it, then they drop due to the conflict, as if they were not revealed originally. When they are removed, the lower proof remains without opposition, so one acts upon it. This is what remains, so ponder. And the Revealer of Divine Secrets (may his secret be sanctified) said: I have seen in some usul books that analogy would require all proofs to be dropped, but consensus has formed on dropping the strong ones and acting upon the lower. If this is established, the matter becomes very easy, so understand. Second aspect: This principle requires that when two verses conflict, one resorts to mutawatir Sunnah; when they conflict, to ijma' if present, otherwise to ahad reports; when they conflict, to sayings of the Companions, then to qiyas. So why do you say that when two verses conflict, one resorts to ahad reports, then to sayings of the Companions and qiyas? The answer to this is: Ijma' is preponderant and precedes everything when it opposes them, because it cannot be abrogated by Book or Sunnah and cannot be invalid, so it is determined that the Book and Sunnah, even if mutawatir, are abrogated, and ijma' reveals the abrogation. So when two verses or two sunnahs conflict and ijma' exists, one acts upon what ijma' agrees with and makes it abrogating what contradicts it, thus it is favored by a definitive preponderance. The discussion is where there is no preference, and there ijma' is not possible. As for mutawatir Sunnah, it is like a verse in necessitating action and definitiveness. So mutawatir Sunnah conflicts with a verse just as another verse conflicts with it, and it is not destroyed by it. If it were nullified due to conflict, it would necessitate dropping all of the two verses and the Sunnah. From this, what is in al-Talwih is rebutted: that considering khabar al-wahid when two verses conflict is either because the report is a preponderant for what agrees with it, so it is objected that there is no preponderance by abundance of proofs, or because the two conflicting proofs drop, so the report remains free from opposition. Something similar can be said.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents two investigations regarding the rule for resolving ta'arud. The first investigation objects that resorting to lower evidence after tasaqut is invalid because the lower evidence itself would be opposed by the conflicting higher evidence. The response from Sheikh al-Haddad argues that two verses are from one speaker (Allah), so their conflict renders them null, leaving the Sunnah unaffected. A second response elaborates that the Sunnah and Book are both divine revelation, and conflict is only apparent. The text then details logical possibilities and concludes that dropping both strong evidences and using lower evidence is supported by consensus, as stated by Mutil' al-Asrar. The second investigation questions why the rule specifies ahad reports after verses, while logically mutawatir Sunnah should come first. The answer is that ijma' is a definitive preponderant that can abrogate even mutawatir. In the absence of ijma', mutawatir Sunnah is equal to a verse and would also drop, so the order is correct. This lesson refines the earlier rule by justifying the specific hierarchy and responding to criticisms from other works like al-Talwih.

Key Terms
الإجماع (al-ijmā‘): Consensus of the mujtahids of the ummah, which is a definitive proof that can abrogate even mutawatir evidence.
السنة المتواترة (al-sunnah al-mutawātirah): Hadith transmitted by so many narrators that it is definitively true; treated like a Quranic verse in authority.
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wāḥid): A hadith not reaching mutawatir level; probabilistic yet actionable when no stronger evidence exists.
النسخ (al-naskh): Abrogation of a ruling by a later revelation or evidence; ijma' is a kāshif (revealer) of naskh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position defended in this passage, why does resorting to khabar al-wahid after conflict of two verses not require first going to mutawatir sunnah?
A) Because mutawatir sunnah is weaker than Quran
B) Because ijma' may abrogate mutawatir sunnah, and in its absence, mutawatir sunnah would also conflict and drop
C) Because khabar al-wahid is stronger than mutawatir
D) Because the rule is arbitrary from the founder

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi hierarchy after two verses conflict places āḥād reports before mutawātir Sunnah because ijmā‘ can abrogate mutawātir, and without ijmā‘, mutawātir is equal to a verse and also drops.

Resolving Conflict Between Two Quranic Verses: The Hanafi Hierarchy
Defines the Hanafi hierarchy for ta'arud and critiques opposing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi order of recourse when two Quranic verses conflict and evaluate the role of Ijma' as a definitive preponderator.

Core Idea
When two verses conflict, Ijma' is the decisive arbitrator — it cannot be abrogated and indicates which verse abrogates.

This passage addresses a critical point in Hanafi usul: what happens when two Quranic verses appear to contradict each other? The school establishes a clear hierarchy: first consult the mutawatir Sunnah, then seek Ijma', then consider ahad hadith, then companion statements, and finally qiyas. The passage defends this order against objections that ahad hadith should be consulted sooner.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflict and the initial hierarchy
Recognize that the text first presents the principle: when two verses conflict, one turns to mutawatir Sunnah; when they conflict, to Ijma'; then to ahad hadith; then to companion sayings; then to qiyas.
This is the baseline Hanafi order.
2
Analyze the objection and the response about Ijma'
The objection asks why not go directly to ahad hadith. The response: Ijma' is a definitive preponderator that cannot be abrogated; it resolves the conflict by indicating which verse is abrogated (naskh). So if Ijma' exists, it takes priority.
Ijma' is considered stronger than a single verse or hadith in epistemic weight.
3
Examine the treatment of mutawatir Sunnah
Mutawatir Sunnah is equivalent to the Quran in strength. If two verses conflict, and there is no Ijma', the mutawatir Sunnah also falls because it is of equal strength. So one then moves to weaker evidence like ahad hadith.
No preponderance can be achieved between two strong proofs that are equal in epistemic certainty.
4
Refute the alternative views from al-Talwih and Sheikh Hidad
The text criticizes the idea that ahad hadith can preponderate simply by agreeing with one verse, or that only same-type evidence falls. The correct view: all conflicting strong evidences fall, and weaker evidence (even of a different type) is then used.
This refutes the notion that a third verse of the same type would be used instead; the text insists on dropping all strong evidences.
5
Apply the principle: verse vs mutawatir Sunnah conflict
If a verse and a mutawatir Sunnah conflict, both fall (no preponderance), and one turns to the next in hierarchy (ahad hadith, etc.).
This is a unique Hanafi position that differs from some other schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشق الثاني: أن هذا الأصل يقتضي أن يصار عند تعارض الآيتين إلى السنة المتواترة، وعند تعارضها إلى الإجماع إن وجد وإلا فإلى أخبار الآحاد وعند تعارضها إلى أقوال الصحابة، ثم إلى القياس فلم قلتم إنه يصار عند تعارض الآيتين إلى أخبار الآحاد، ثم إلى أقوال الصحابة والقياس؟ والجواب عنه : أن الإجماع مرجح ومقدم على الكل عند معارضته إياها : لأنه لا يكون منسوخاً بكتاب أو سنة ولا يكون باطلاً، فتعين أن يكون الكتاب والسنة ولو كانت متواترة، منسوخة، والإجماع كاشف عن النسخ. فعند تعارض الآيتين أو السنتين ووجود الإجماع يعمل بما وافقه الإجماع ويجعل ناسخاً لما خالفه، فقد ترجح بترجيح قطعي، والكلام فيما لا ترجيح فيه، ولا يمكن هناك الإجماع، وأما السنة المتواترة فمثل الآية في إيجاب العمل والقطعية، فالسنة المتواترة تعارض الآية كما تعارضها آية أخرى ولا تضمحل عندها، فلو أهدر للتعارض يلزم تساقط الكل من الآيتين والسنة ومن ههنا اندفع ما في «التلويح» أن اعتبار خبر الواحد عند تعارض الآيتين إما لأن الخبر مرجح لما وافقه فيرد عليه أن لا ترجيح بكثرة الأدلة، وإما لأن المتعارضين تساقطا، فبقي الخبر سالماً عن المعارضة، فمثله يمكن أن يقال فيما إذا كان آية ثالثة موافقة لإحداهما ، فيقال : قد سقط المتعارضان فيعمل بالثالثة، ولا يحتاج إلى ما أجاب بجواب فاسد هو أن خبر الواحد لما كان ضعيفاً غير معتبر في مقابلة الآية صار تبعاً للآية الموافقة له، فيصلح مرجحاً، وقد عرفت فساده، ولا يحتاج أيضاً إلى ما أجاب به الشيخ الهداد، أن التساقط إنما هو للدليلين من نوع واحد، ويعمل بنوع آخر، والآية الثالثة من نوع المتعارضين، وقد عرفت فساده بوجه آخر أيضاً ومما بينا ظهر لك أن الآية والسنة المتواترة إذا تعارضتا تسقطان أيضاً لا كما زعمه الشيخ الهداد فافهم.
The second branch: This principle requires that when two verses conflict, one should turn to the mutawatir Sunnah; when they conflict, to Ijma' if found, otherwise to ahad hadith; when they conflict, to the sayings of the Companions; then to qiyas. So why do you say that when two verses conflict, one turns to ahad hadith, then to sayings of the Companions and qiyas? The answer: Ijma' is preponderant and foremost over all when it opposes them, because it cannot be abrogated by a Book or Sunnah, nor is it invalid. So it is determined that the Book and Sunnah, even if mutawatir, can be abrogated, and Ijma' reveals abrogation. So when two verses or two sunnahs conflict and Ijma' exists, one acts upon what agrees with Ijma' and considers it abrogating what contradicts it, for it is preponderated by a definite preponderance. The discussion is where there is no preponderance, and Ijma' is not possible there. As for the mutawatir Sunnah, it is like the verse in requiring action and definitiveness. So the mutawatir Sunnah conflicts with a verse just as another verse conflicts with it, and it is not overwhelmed by it. So if it were discarded due to conflict, that would entail the complete fall of both verses and the Sunnah. From here, what is in al-Talwih is refuted: that considering khabar al-wahid when two verses conflict is either because the report preponderates what agrees with it—but it is replied that there is no preponderance by multitude of proofs—or because the two conflicting proofs collapse, leaving the report safe from opposition; the like of which can be said when there is a third verse agreeing with one of them: it would be said that the two conflicting ones have fallen, so they act upon the third. There is no need for the corrupt answer that because khabar al-wahid is weak and not considered in the face of a verse, it becomes dependent on the verse agreeing with it, so it can serve as a preponderator—and you have known its corruption. There is also no need for what Sheikh Hidad answered: that the fall is only for two proofs of the same type, and they act upon another type, while the third verse is of the same type as the conflicting ones—and you have known its corruption from another aspect. And from what we have clarified, it appears to you that when a verse and a mutawatir Sunnah conflict, they also fall, as Sheikh Hidad claimed, so understand.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on ta'arud by addressing a specific issue: what is the hierarchy of sources when two Quranic verses conflict? The author presents the Hanafi position: one first turns to mutawatir Sunnah, then to Ijma' (consensus), then to ahad hadith, then to the sayings of the Companions, and finally to qiyas. Then he addresses an objection: why do some say that ahad hadith are consulted directly after the verses? The defense is that Ijma' is a definitive preponderator; it cannot be abrogated by a single verse or hadith, but it can indicate the abrogation of one. Therefore, when Ijma' exists, it resolves the conflict. In the absence of Ijma', the mutawatir Sunnah is equal to the Quran in strength, so if verses conflict, the mutawatir Sunnah also falls, leaving room for weaker evidence. The text refutes the view from al-Talwih and Sheikh Hidad, asserting that the correct approach is to drop both strong conflicting evidences and act upon the weaker, regardless of type. This is a key Hanafi principle for resolving conflicts.

Key Terms
سنۃ متواترة (sunna mutawatira): Sunnah that has been transmitted by so many narrators that it yields certain knowledge.
إجماع (ijma): Consensus of the scholars of the ummah, which is an infallible proof.
خبر واحد (khabar wahid): A hadith narrated by one or a few narrators, yielding probable knowledge.
تعارض (ta'arud): Conflict or apparent contradiction between two proofs.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation of a earlier ruling by a later one; here Ijma' can reveal naskh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first source to turn to when two Quranic verses conflict and no tarjih is possible?
A) The mutawatir Sunnah.
B) The ahad hadith.
C) The sayings of the Companions.
D) Qiyas.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, Ijma' serves as the definitive arbiter when two strong proofs (verses or mutawatir Sunnah) conflict, overriding the need for ahad hadith.

Resolving Conflicting Hadith: Su'r al-Himar
Analyzing the Hanafi response to conflicting hadith on su'r al-himar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi method of resolving conflicting hadith by identifying the role of naskh and the precautionary combination (jam' bayn al-dalilayn) in cases where abrogation is not clear.

Core Idea
When hadith conflict, Hanafis first seek naskh; if not, they combine both proofs with precaution.

This passage delves into a classic usul challenge: how to rule when two hadiths appear to contradict each other. The Hanafi school employs a structured approach—first checking for abrogation (naskh), and if that cannot be determined, combining both evidences through precautionary measures. By studying the case of the donkey's leftover water, you will learn how to systematically resolve textual conflicts within the Hanafi framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting proofs
Locate the hadith of permissibility (e.g., allowing riding the donkey) and the hadith of prohibition (forbidding the riding, implying impurity). These form the apparent ta'āruḍ (conflict).
The passage mentions the hadith of riding as evidence of impurity.
2
Apply the naskh principle
Determine if one hadith is later and can abrogate the other. The prohibition hadith is argued to be the nasikh (abrogator) because it mentions riding, which indicates impurity, and is considered later.
If naskh is established, no conflict remains—the later ruling prevails.
3
If naskh is rejected, apply istishab and precaution
If the opponent does not accept naskh, the Hanafi method disregards both proofs as if no revelation came. Then return to original principles (istishab): water is originally pure, the body is originally impure (hadath). This requires using water (based on original purity) but also tayammum to ensure removal of hadath due to lingering possibility of impurity.
This is not dropping proofs but combining them for certainty.
4
Evaluate the outcome
The ruling: use the water (since purity is presumed) and combine tayammum (to ensure hadath removal). This precautionary combination (jam') ensures the obligated person exits his duty with certainty.
This is a valid ijtihad, not a claim of being the only correct ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا يتنجس بالشك ولا يطهر المتوضىء لأنه كان محدثاً في الأصل، فلا يزول بالشك، فبقي كما كان ذلك احتمال زوال الحدث قائم فوجب استعمال الماء وضم التيمم كذا قالوا مع ولا يرد عليه أن الحرمة يجوز أن تكون للكرامة وليس الحل من لوازم الطهارة قطعاً، لأن التعليل بكونه الركوب مذكور في حديث التحريم فلا احتمال للكرامة . وههنا بحث، فإن حديث الحرمة ناسخ لحديث الحل فلا تعارض أصلاً، ولأجل ذلك غير الشيخ ابن الهمام وقال : التحريم يدل على النجاسة والضرورة توجب الطهارة فقد تعارضا. وفيه أولاً : أن الطهارة حينئذ ثبتت بالتعليل والنجاسة بالنص فلا تعارض، وثانياً : المعتبر الضرورة الشديدة كما في الهرة، وقد مر وليست فالأولى أن يقال : عارضه حديث الركوب على الحمار، ولا يخلو من المخالطة بالعرق ولا قياس وبحث آخر هو أن الماء لما كان طاهراً عملاً بالأصل فلا بد من استعماله لإزالة الحدث ولا وجه لضم التيمم، كيف لا ومعنى تقرير الأصول أن يهدر الحجتان ويعمل بالأصل، وإذا هدر الحجتان صار الحادثة كأن لم ينزل فيها شيء، والماء كان في الأصل طاهراً فيبقى على طهارته، فإذا لاقى العضو أزال الحدث فلم يبق في اليد شيء يحكم بضم التيمم إلا الاحتياط بقيام احتمال بقاء النجاسة في الماء، فقام احتمال عدم زوال الحدث، ثم ليس مقتضى الاحتياط ضم التيمم، لأنه وإن كان مزيلاً للحدث لكنه ليس مزيلاً للخبث واستعمال هذا الماء كما أقام احتمال عدم زوال الحدث أقام احتمال تنجس الأعضاء فالتيمم لا يغني بل مقتضى الاحتياط لو اعتبر اراقة الماء ثم التيمم وهذا الإشكال وإن استصعبه الأذكياء لكن يراه هذا العبد سهل الاندفاع، فإنا سلمنا أن تقرير الأصول يقتضي إهدار الحجتين من البين وأن الحادثة كأنها لم ينزل فيها شيء إلا أنهما كما تدلان على نجاسة الماء وطهارته كذلك تدلان على زوال الحدث باستعماله وعدم زواله، فإذا أهدرا كان كأن لم ينزل في النجاسة والطهارة شيء وكذا في زوال الحدث وعدمه، كأنه لم ينزل شيء، والأصل في الماء الطهارة، فحكم بها، والأصل في البدن الحدث، فحكم به وبعدم زواله باستعمال هذا الماء، وكيف وليس الحكم بزوال الحدث إلا أمراً تعبدياً بإبانة الشرع، وإذا أهدر الحجتان ارتفع من البين والتيمم عرف مزيلاً فوجب. ثم إن الماء الطاهر موجود البتة . واحتمال إزالته قائم فوجب استعماله للاحتياط، فاستعمال الماء للاحتياط، وأما ضم التيمم فأمر حتم، ولما حكمنا بسقوط الحجتين وحكمنا ببقاء طهارة الماء بالأصل، فاحتمال نجاسته كاحتمال وقوع النجاسة في ماء موضوع من الليل، وهذا الاحتمال مهدر رعاً، فلا وجه للاحتياط بالإراقة فافهم، فقد ظهر لك مما قررنا أن الشك ههنا في الطهورية أو في الطهارة، ثم أورد بحث آخر هو أن الحجتين إذ قد تعارضتا فواحدة منهما منسوخة في نفس الأمر أو باطلة، فلا حكم في نفس الأمر إلا لواحدة، فالإشكال في السؤر ليس في الشارع، بل منا بجهلنا، فهذا الحكم منالاً من الشارع ولك أن تجيب عنه أنه ليس المقصود أن قد أشكل بل المقصود أنه لا بد ههنا من تقرير الأصول وهو يقتضي أن يكون الحكم كذا، ويظن أن حكم الشارع في سؤر الحمار والبغل استعمال الماء وضم التيمم، وهذا أمر ممكن لا يأبى عنه العقل وهدى إليه الدليل، وأما أنه صواب مطابق فلا ندعيه، بل هو كسائر الاجتهادات، بل لا يبعد أن يقال: إن الحكم من الشارع كفاية أحدهما من الوضوء به أو التيمم ، لكن إذا كان الأمر مشكوكاً عندنا حكمنا بهما ليخرج المكلف عن العهدة بيقين فتأمل فيه، وقد ينقض بالضب، فإن أحاديث الحل والحرمة قد تعارضت هناك أيضاً، وجوابه أن القياس هناك على السباع ممكن لوجود العلة المشتركة بخلاف الحمار هذا غاية كلام في هذا المقام، وبقي كلام طويل فاطلبه من المطولات.
So it does not become impure by doubt, nor does the one performing wudu become purified, because he was originally in a state of hadath, so it is not removed by doubt. Thus it remains as it was, and the possibility of removal of hadath exists, so it is necessary to use water and combine tayammum. This is what they said. And it is not objected that the prohibition may be for honor, and permissibility is not a necessary concomitant of purity, definitely, because the reasoning by riding is mentioned in the hadith of prohibition, so there is no possibility of honor. And here is a discussion: Indeed, the hadith of prohibition abrogates the hadith of permissibility, so there is no conflict at all. For that reason, Shaykh Ibn al-Humam changed and said: The prohibition indicates impurity, and necessity necessitates purification, so they have conflicted. In it, first: then purification is established by reasoning and impurity by text, so there is no conflict. Second: what is considered is severe necessity like the cat, and it has passed, and it is not so. The better course is to say: the hadith of riding the donkey opposes it, and it is not free from mixing with sweat, and there is no analogy. And another discussion is that since the water is pure by acting on the original principle, it must be used to remove hadath, and there is no reason to combine tayammum. How not? The meaning of establishing the principles is that both proofs are disregarded and the original principle is acted upon. When both proofs are disregarded, the incident becomes as if nothing had been revealed concerning it. The water was originally pure, so it remains on its purity. When it contacts the limb, it removes hadath, so nothing remains in the hand that necessitates combining tayammum except precaution due to the possibility of remaining impurity in the water, so the possibility of non-removal of hadath stands. Then the requirement of precaution is not to combine tayammum, because although it removes hadath, it does not remove filth. And using this water, as it raised the possibility of non-removal of hadath, also raised the possibility of impurity of the limbs. So tayammum does not suffice; rather, the requirement of precaution, if considered, would be to throw away the water and then do tayammum. And this difficulty, though the intelligent find it difficult, this servant sees it as easy to resolve. For we admit that establishing the principles requires disregarding both proofs from the matter, and the incident is as if nothing had been revealed concerning it. However, just as they indicate the impurity and purity of the water, they also indicate the removal of hadath by using it and its non-removal. When they are disregarded, it is as if nothing had been revealed concerning impurity and purity, and similarly concerning removal of hadath and its non-removal, as if nothing had been revealed. The original principle for water is purity, so it is ruled accordingly. The original principle for the body is hadath, so it is ruled accordingly and that it is not removed by using this water. How can it be? The removal of hadath is only a devotional matter by the indication of the Law. When both proofs are disregarded, it is removed from the matter, and tayammum is known to be a remover, so it became obligatory. Then the pure water certainly exists, and the possibility of its removal exists, so its use is obligatory for precaution. So using water is for precaution, and combining tayammum is a necessary matter. Since we ruled that both proofs are dropped and we ruled for the continuation of the purity of water by the original principle, the possibility of its impurity is like the possibility of impurity falling into water placed from the night. This possibility is disregarded, so there is no justification for precaution by throwing it away. Understand this. So it has become clear to you from what we have stated that the doubt here is concerning purifiability or purity. Then another discussion is presented: Indeed, when the two proofs have conflicted, one of them is abrogated in reality or invalid. So there is no ruling in reality except for one. So the difficulty concerning the su'r is not from the Lawgiver, but from us due to our ignorance. So this ruling is received from the Lawgiver. And you may answer that the intended point is not that it is problematic, but rather that here it is necessary to establish the principles, and this requires that the ruling be such. And it is thought that the ruling of the Lawgiver concerning the su'r of the donkey and mule is to use water and combine tayammum, and this is something possible, not rejected by intellect, and the evidence leads to it. As for it being correct and conforming, we do not claim that; rather, it is like other ijtihads. In fact, it is not far-fetched to say: The ruling from the Lawgiver is sufficiency of one of them: wudu with it or tayammum. But when the matter is doubtful to us, we rule with both to remove the responsibility of the obligated person with certainty. Reflect on this. And it may be refuted by the lizard (dabb), for the hadiths of permissibility and prohibition have conflicted there as well. The answer is that analogy there with predatory animals is possible due to the existence of the common cause, unlike the donkey. This is the extent of the discussion in this place, and there remains a long discussion, so seek it from the lengthy works.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi usul analysis of conflicting textual evidence regarding the water leftover (su'r) of donkeys and mules. The Hanafi position is that the water is impure because a later prohibition hadith abrogated an earlier permissibility hadith. The text first addresses an objection: perhaps the prohibition was for honor, not impurity. The response: the hadith mentions riding, so it indicates impurity. Then a discussion follows about the proper method when two proofs appear to conflict without clear abrogation. The author explains that Hanafis do not simply disregard both proofs but apply usul principles: they retain the original purity of water (istishab) but also require tayammum as a precaution because there remains a possibility of impurity. The text refutes the idea that this is contradictory or arbitrary, showing that it is a valid ijtihad based on the need to ensure certainty in discharging one's obligation. Finally, the author contrasts this with the case of the lizard (dabb), where analogy with wild animals is possible, unlike the donkey, which has no analogous case. The passage demonstrates the Hanafi commitment to combining evidences (jama') rather than dropping them, and the role of istihsan in resolving apparent conflicts.

Key Terms
السؤر (al-su'r): The water leftover (remaining) in a vessel after an animal or person drinks, or the remnants.
الناسخ (al-nasikh): The later legal evidence that abrogates a previous ruling.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Preferring a ruling that is more suitable based on a stronger evidence or necessity, departing from strict qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on using the water leftover (su'r) of a donkey for wudu when hadiths conflict?
A) The water is pure and wudu is valid without tayammum.
B) The water is impure and wudu is invalid; only tayammum is required.
C) Both water and tayammum must be used out of precaution.
D) The water may be used but must be followed by tayammum.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi method for conflicting hadith is to seek naskh first; if not possible, combine both proofs through precaution (istishab and tayammum) to ensure certainty.

Al-Ijma — Consensus
intermediate
8 min
No Preponderance by Multiplicity of Evidences
Presents the Hanafi principle and refutes opposing arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on tarjih by multiplicity and apply it to distinguish between independent proofs and socially configured evidence.

Core Idea
Multiplicity of proofs does not strengthen them; each is independent and they cancel out unless forming a tawatur-like social configuration.

This lesson examines a fundamental Hanafi usul principle: that mere multiplicity of evidences or narrators does not confer preponderance (tarjih) over a single proof. While the majority of scholars hold that more proofs gradually strengthen the probability, the Hanafis argue that each proof stands alone and they cancel out in conflict. This principle has far-reaching implications for handling conflicting evidences in fiqh and usul.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi rule
Identify the precise formulation: 'No tarjih by multiplicity of evidences and narrators unless reaching shuhra/tawatur.'
This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; Imam Muhammad differs.
2
Understand the rationale
Grasp the key premise: each evidence is independent (mustaqill) and sufficient on its own. In a conflict, they cancel out rather than combine.
The analogy of testimony: two witnesses against two others cancel, not outweigh.
3
Examine the Hanafi proofs
Work through the three analogies: (1) testimony, (2) inheritance: cousin with dual kinship does not exclude a single cousin, (3) cousin-husband case. Also consensus of companions (save Ibn Mas'ud).
These show that multiplicity does not create strength.
4
Recognize the exception
Note that when multiplicity takes on a social configuration (hay'a ijtima'iyya) as in mutawatir and mashhur, it does yield strength. This is because the individual reports alone are insufficient without the collective.
This exception preserves the strength of tawatur and shuhra without contradicting the rule for independent proofs.
5
Engage with the counterargument
The majority argue that suspicion (zann) strengthens gradually with more reporters, similar to tawatur. The Hanafi response is that this logic applies to narrators (riwaya) not to independent proofs (dalil), and that certainties cannot have degrees.
Understand the distinction between cumulative evidence and independent proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة: (لا ترجيح بكثرة الأدلة والرواة ما لم تبلغ حد الشهرة عند) الإمامين (أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف، خلافاً للأكثر) هم الأئمة الثلاثة والإمام محمد لهما قيام المعارضة مع كل دلیل) فإن كل واحد واحد دلیل مستقل فمعارض واحد كما يعارضه يعارض آخر أيضاً (فيسقط الكل) عند المعارضة، فلا وجه للترجيح (كالشهادة) فإن شهادة الاثنين كما تعارض شهادة اثنين آخرين كذلك تعارض شهادة أربعة (و) لهما أيضاً إجماع من سوى ابن مسعود) من الصحابة ومن بعدهم على عدم ترجيح ابن عم هو أخ لأم على من هو ابن عم فقط) الميراث (فلا يكون الأول (حاجباً) للثاني بل يستحق بكل قرابة مستقلاً) فكذا الأدلة الكثيرة التي كل منها سبب للعلم لا تترجح على الواحد (و) لهما أيضاً (إجماع الكل على عدمه أي عدم الترجيح (في ابن عم حال كونه (زوجاً على ابن عم فقط) مع وجود القرابتين فكذا ههنا نعم لو كان هناك كثرة لها هيئة اجتماعية موجبة لما لا آحادها استقلالاً، يوجب ويكون إفادة الأحاد مشروطاً بهذه الهيئة أفادت قوة زائدة البتة كما في المتواتر والمشهور (ولا يخفى على الفطن ضعف هذه الوجوه أما ضعف الأول فلأن الكل معاً يفيد قوة الثبوت، ألا ترى أن زيداً يقاوم كل أحد ولا يقاوم الكل، وأما الثاني والثالث فإنما يتمان لو كان كل من جهتي القرابة يقتضي العصوبة وليس كذلك فإن الزوجية إذا انفردت تقتضي استحقاق النصف لا غير، وكذلك الأخوة لأم لو انفردت اقتضت استحقاق السدس كذا في «الحاشية». وأنت لا يذهب عليك أن محصول الدليل الأول أن كل واحد من الأدلة ملزوم حصول النتيجة، ففي إعطاء النتيجة، ففي إعطاء النتيجة كل كاف وهي كما تحصل من الدليل الواحد كذلك الدليلين، فلا يحصل من الاجتماع قوة زائدة فانحسم المنع ، ومن ههنا يسمع في المناظرة أن جواب المعارضة لا يصح بالمعارضة الأخرى، واعتبر من نفسك لو كان اجتماع الدلائل يوجب قوة زائدة لما تكثير الدلائل على القطعيات فإنها لا تقبل القوة والضعف، وإلا لم تبق صح قطعیات نعم عند كثرة الرواة للتشكيك ،مجال فإن الهيئة الاجتماعية العارضة تفيد قوة لم تكن من قبل فافهم ومحصول الثاني والثالث أن قرابة العصوبة والزوجية أو الأخوة لأم كل كان ملزوماً لاستحقاق الميراث وإن لم يكن كل منها عصوبة ولا يحصل باجتماعها قوة زائدة، فكذا الدلائل كل منها لما كان مفيداً للنتيجة بالاستقلال، فلا يحصل بالاجتماع قوة زائدة فافهم. (وللجمهور أن الظن يتقوى بتدريج بكثرة المخبرين (حتى ينتهي إلى اليقين بالتواتر فالكثرة مفيدة للقوة فتترجح ولا يذهب عليك أن هذا لا يجري في كثرة الأدلة، فإن التقوي بالتدريج فيه ممنوع فالدليل قاصر على الدعوى، ثم رواة: «إنما الماء الماء» كانت من من
The issue: There is no preponderance by multiplicity of evidences and narrators unless they reach the level of renown (shuhra) according to the two imams Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, contrary to the majority. They are the three imams (the other schools) and Imam Muhammad is with them (the position) that each evidence stands in opposition with every other evidence. For each single evidence is an independent proof, so a single contradicting evidence contradicts it, and another also contradicts it, so all fall in case of conflict, so there is no basis for tarjih. Like testimony: the testimony of two witnesses, just as it is opposed by two other witnesses, so also is the testimony of four opposed by four. And they also have a consensus of all except Ibn Mas'ud from the companions and after them on not giving tarjih to a cousin who is also a brother from the mother over a cousin only in inheritance. So the former does not prevent (hajb) the latter, rather each is entitled by each kinship independently. So likewise, multiple proofs each of which is a cause for knowledge do not become preponderant over a single proof. And they also have the consensus of all on its non-existence, i.e., no tarjih, in the case of a cousin who is also a husband over a cousin only, with the existence of two kinships. So here as well. Yes, if there is multiplicity that has a social configuration that necessitates a result that its individuals independently do not, and the benefit of the individuals is conditioned by this configuration, then it yields an additional strength, as in mutawatir and mashhur. And it is not hidden from the intelligent the weakness of these proofs. As for the weakness of the first: because the whole together yields strength of establishment; do you not see that Zayd can resist any individual but cannot resist the whole? As for the second and third: they are only valid if each of the two kinship relations entails being an 'asaba (male agnate), but that is not the case: when only the marital relation exists, it entails entitlement to half only, and likewise brotherhood from mother alone entails one-sixth. As stated in the Hashiya. And it does not escape you that the gist of the first proof is that each evidence is necessarily productive of the result; in producing the result, each is sufficient, just as it is obtained from one proof, so from two, so no additional strength comes from combination; thus the prohibition is resolved. From this, it is heard in debate that responding to an objection with another objection is not valid. And consider for yourself: if the combination of proofs necessitated additional strength, then why are proofs multiplied for certainties? For certainties do not admit strength and weakness; otherwise certainties would not remain. Yes, when there is a multiplicity of narrators for a possibility, then the social configuration that arises yields strength not previously present, so understand. And the gist of the second and third: the kinship of 'asaba, marital relation, or brotherhood from mother, each is necessarily productive of entitlement to inheritance, even if each alone is not 'asaba, and their combination does not yield additional strength. So likewise, proofs each is independently productive of the result, so combination does not yield additional strength. Understand. And for the majority: that suspicion strengthens gradually with the increase of reporters until it reaches certainty through tawatur; so multiplicity is beneficial for strength and thus becomes preponderant. And it does not escape you that this does not apply to multiplicity of proofs, for gradual strengthening is denied there. So the proof is insufficient for the claim. Then the narrators of 'Innama al-ma'u al-ma' (water is water) were from...
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage establishes a key Hanafi usul principle: mere multiplicity of evidences or narrators does not confer preponderance unless it reaches tawatur or shuhra. The text presents the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, while Imam Muhammad aligns with the majority (including Shafi'i) that multiplicity does give tarjih. The Hanafi argument uses analogy: just as testimony of two does not outweigh two others, and in inheritance law, a cousin who is also a brother from the mother does not take precedence over a cousin only, similarly multiple proofs do not outweigh a single one. The author then critiques the majority's argument that multiple reporters increase suspicion (zann) gradually, stating that this applies to narrators but not to independent proofs. The text concludes that social configuration (hay'a ijtima'iyya) gives strength in cases of tawatur, but not for individual proofs.

Key Terms
ترجيح (tarjih): Preponderance given to one conflicting evidence over another.
كثرة الأدلة (kathrat al-adilla): Multiplicity of proofs or evidences; according to Hanafis, this does not give tarjih unless meeting shuhra.
الشهرة (al-shuhra): Renown; a level of multiplicity that entails additional strength, below tawatur.
الهيئة الاجتماعية (al-hay'a al-ijtima'iyya): Social configuration; a collective structure of multiple reports that yields strength not present in individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, multiplicity of independent proofs does not give tarjih; only when they form a social configuration (shuhra/tawatur) does strength increase.

Al-Ijma — Consensus
intermediate

The Hanafi Position: No Tarjih by Multiplicity Alone
Presentation of the Imamayn's position and three proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on tarjih by multiplicity and explain the three proofs of the Imamayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf).

Core Idea
Multiplicity of proofs does not strengthen the result; each proof is independent and sufficient alone.

In this passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut, the author presents the core Hanafi position that mere abundance of proofs or narrators does not confer preference in conflict. The Imamayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) provide three analogies—testimony, inheritance cases—to demonstrate that adding more of the same kind of evidence adds no additional strength. Only when multiplicity reaches a level where a social structure (hay'a ijtima'iyya) emerges, as in tawatur, does it give extra weight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
Read the opening statement: 'There is no preference by abundance of proofs and narrators as long as they do not reach the level of fame (shuhra) according to the two Imams Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, contrary to the majority.' This is the core thesis.
Note the exception: shuhra can give preference, but not mere multiplicity.
2
Examine the first proof: analogy to testimony
The Imamayn argue that just as the testimony of two witnesses opposes the testimony of two others, and four oppose four, without preference, so multiple proofs oppose each other and all fall. Each proof is independent.
The key is independence: each proof alone is sufficient for the result.
3
Examine the second proof: consensus on inheritance
There is consensus (except Ibn Mas'ud) that a cousin who is also a maternal brother does not block a pure cousin; each kinship gives independent entitlement. Similarly, each proof gives the result independently.
This shows that multiple relations do not combine to give extra strength.
4
Examine the third proof: another inheritance consensus
Similarly, a cousin who is also a husband does not block a pure cousin. Even with two relations, no extra strength is gained.
Reinforces the same principle.
5
Understand the qualification for social structure
The Imamayn acknowledge that if multiplicity has a social structure (hay'a ijtima'iyya) that gives something beyond the individuals, as in mutawatir and mashhur, it does give additional strength. But this is not mere multiplicity; it is a qualitative change.
This exception is critical: it shows the Hanafi view is nuanced, not absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان
حكم الكل في مواضع منها هذا بدليل تصحيح صوم عاشوراء المنوي في اليوم حين فرضاً وهذا ذاتي) لأنه بالأجزاء، بخلاف الترجيح بالعبادة لكونه بالعرضي (أقول في كون العبادة وصفاً عرضياً للحقيقة الشرعية للصوم نظر فتدبر ) بل لا يصح على رأينا، لأن الصوم عبادة عندنا عما اعتبره الشارع ،عبادة كما مر في مسائل النهي بل الوجه أنه لم يوجد ههنا مفسد الكل أصلاً، فإن القدر المشروط من النية قد وجد.

مع وجود
المسألة: (لا ترجيح بكثرة الأدلة والرواة ما لم تبلغ حد الشهرة عند) الإمامين (أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف، خلافاً للأكثر) هم الأئمة الثلاثة والإمام محمد لهما قيام المعارضة مع كل دلیل) فإن كل واحد واحد دلیل مستقل فمعارض واحد كما يعارضه يعارض آخر أيضاً (فيسقط الكل) عند المعارضة، فلا وجه للترجيح (كالشهادة) فإن شهادة الاثنين كما تعارض شهادة اثنين آخرين كذلك تعارض شهادة أربعة (و) لهما أيضاً إجماع من سوى ابن مسعود) من الصحابة ومن بعدهم على عدم ترجيح ابن عم هو أخ لأم على من هو ابن عم فقط) الميراث (فلا يكون الأول (حاجباً) للثاني بل يستحق بكل قرابة مستقلاً) فكذا الأدلة الكثيرة التي كل منها سبب للعلم لا تترجح على الواحد (و) لهما أيضاً (إجماع الكل على عدمه أي عدم الترجيح (في ابن عم حال كونه (زوجاً على ابن عم فقط) مع وجود القرابتين فكذا ههنا نعم لو كان هناك كثرة لها هيئة اجتماعية موجبة لما لا آحادها استقلالاً، يوجب ويكون إفادة الأحاد مشروطاً بهذه الهيئة أفادت قوة زائدة البتة كما في المتواتر والمشهور (ولا يخفى على الفطن ضعف هذه الوجوه أما ضعف الأول فلأن الكل معاً يفيد قوة الثبوت، ألا ترى أن زيداً يقاوم كل أحد ولا يقاوم الكل، وأما الثاني والثالث فإنما يتمان لو كان كل من جهتي القرابة يقتضي العصوبة وليس كذلك فإن الزوجية إذا انفردت تقتضي استحقاق النصف لا غير، وكذلك الأخوة لأم لو انفردت اقتضت استحقاق السدس كذا في «الحاشية». وأنت لا يذهب عليك أن محصول الدليل الأول أن كل واحد من الأدلة ملزوم حصول النتيجة، ففي إعطاء النتيجة، ففي إعطاء النتيجة كل كاف وهي كما تحصل من الدليل الواحد كذلك الدليلين، فلا يحصل من الاجتماع قوة زائدة فانحسم المنع ، ومن ههنا يسمع في المناظرة أن جواب المعارضة لا يصح بالمعارضة الأخرى، واعتبر من نفسك لو كان اجتماع الدلائل يوجب قوة زائدة لما تكثير الدلائل على القطعيات فإنها لا تقبل القوة والضعف، وإلا لم تبق صح قطعیات نعم عند كثرة الرواة للتشكيك ،مجال فإن الهيئة الاجتماعية العارضة تفيد قوة لم تكن من قبل فافهم ومحصول الثاني والثالث أن قرابة العصوبة والزوجية أو الأخوة لأم كل كان ملزوماً لاستحقاق الميراث وإن لم يكن كل منها عصوبة ولا يحصل باجتماعها قوة زائدة، فكذا الدلائل كل منها لما كان مفيداً للنتيجة بالاستقلال، فلا يحصل بالاجتماع قوة زائدة فافهم. (وللجمهور أن الظن يتقوى بتدريج بكثرة المخبرين (حتى ينتهي إلى اليقين بالتواتر فالكثرة مفيدة للقوة فتترجح ولا يذهب عليك أن هذا لا يجري في كثرة الأدلة، فإن التقوي بالتدريج فيه ممنوع فالدليل قاصر على الدعوى، ثم رواة: «إنما الماء الماء» كانت من
من
أكثر وقد رجح أمير المؤمنين عمر وغيره من الصحابة خبر أم المؤمنين وحدها، فلم يعتبروا التقوّي بكثرة الرواة، ولعل هذه القوة ضعيفة مراتبها متفاوتة ففي اعتبارها نوع من العسر والله يريد اليسر (أقول منقوض هذا بكثرة الاجتهاد، فإن عدم الترجيح بها اتفاق بيننا وبينكم (مع أنه ينتهي إلى اليقين بالاجماع كما أنه تنتهي كثرة الرواة بالتواتر إلى القطع (فتدبر) وجوابه أن القطع الحاصل بالاجماع تعبدي يحصل دفعة لا بأن يتدرج الظن الحاصل باجتهاد إلى أن يتقوّى بانضمام آخر حتى ينتهي بالإجماع إلى اليقين بخلاف الرواة، فإن الظن فيه يتقوى بالانضمام وينتهي بالتواتر إلى اليقين فافهم.
The ruling of the whole in places including this is evidenced by the validity of the fast of 'Ashura' intended during the day as obligatory, and this is essential because it is by parts, unlike preference by worship because it is accidental. I say: There is consideration in worship being an accidental description of the legal reality of fasting, so reflect. Rather, it is not valid in our view, because fasting is worship for us from what the Lawgiver considered worship, as passed in issues of prohibition. Rather, the point is that here there is no corruptor of the whole at all, for the required amount of intention has occurred. The issue: There is no preference by abundance of proofs and narrators as long as they do not reach the level of fame according to the two Imams Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, contrary to the majority (they are the three Imams and Imam Muhammad). For them, each proof is independent, so one proof opposes another, and another also opposes, so all fall when opposed, so there is no basis for preference, like testimony: just as the testimony of two opposes the testimony of two others, so also the testimony of four opposes. And for them also is consensus of all except Ibn Mas'ud among the Companions and those after them on not preferring a cousin who is also a maternal brother over one who is only a cousin in inheritance, so the former does not block the latter, but each kinship gives entitlement independently, similarly the many proofs each of which is a cause of knowledge are not preferred over one. And for them also is consensus of all on its absence, i.e., absence of preference, in the case of a cousin who is also a husband over only a cousin, despite the existence of two relationships, so likewise here. Yes, if there is abundance that has a social structure that necessitates something that individual parts do not independently, and the benefit of the individuals is conditioned by this structure, it gives additional strength undoubtedly, as in the mutawatir and mashhur. And it is not hidden from the intelligent the weakness of these arguments. As for the weakness of the first, the whole together gives strength of substantiation; do you not see that Zayd resists every individual but does not resist the whole? As for the second and third, they are only valid if each of the two kinship relations necessitates 'usuba, but that is not so, for the husband relation alone necessitates entitlement to half only, and maternal brotherhood alone necessitates entitlement to a sixth, as in al-Hashiya. And it does not escape you that the gist of the first argument is that each proof is entailing the result; in giving the result, each is sufficient, and just as it obtains from one proof, so from two, so no additional strength is obtained from combination, so the prevention is cut off. Hence it is heard in debate that the answer to one objection is not valid by another objection. And consider from yourself: if combination of proofs necessitated additional strength, then the abundance of proofs for certainties would have no meaning, for they do not accept strength and weakness; otherwise certainties would not remain sound. Yes, with abundance of narrators there is room for doubt, for the occasional social structure gives strength that was not there before, so understand. The gist of the second and third is that the kinship of 'usuba and husband or maternal brotherhood each is entailing entitlement to inheritance even if each is not 'usuba, and their combination gives no additional strength, so likewise the proofs, each being independently beneficial for the result, their combination gives no additional strength, so understand. And for the majority that assumption is gradually strengthened by abundance of informants until it reaches certainty by tawatur, so multiplicity gives strength, so preference is given. And it does not escape you that this does not apply to abundance of proofs, for gradual strengthening in it is denied, so the proof falls short of the claim. Then the narrators of 'Innama al-ma'u al-ma'u' were many, and Amir al-Mu'minin 'Umar and other Companions gave preference to the single report of the Mother of the Believers, and they did not consider strengthening by abundance of narrators; perhaps this strength is weak and its levels vary, so considering it involves some difficulty, and Allah desires ease. I say: This is refuted by abundance of ijtihad, for its lack of preference is agreed between us and you, even though it reaches certainty by consensus just as abundance of narrators reaches certainty by tawatur, so reflect. The answer is that the certainty obtained by consensus is devotional, obtained suddenly, not by gradual strengthening of the assumption from ijtihad by addition until it reaches certainty by consensus, unlike narrators, for assumption in them strengthens by addition and reaches certainty by tawatur, so understand.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut discusses a dispute in usul al-fiqh regarding whether abundance of proofs (kathrat al-adilla) or narrators (kathrat al-ruwat) gives additional strength for tarjih (preference) when conflicting evidences are present. The issue is: if one has multiple proofs on one side and a single proof on the other, does the multiplicity give added weight? The Hanafi position, according to Imam al-A'zam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf (the Imamayn), is that there is no preference by mere multiplicity unless the evidence reaches the level of shuhra (fame). The majority (including Imam Muhammad, al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad) hold that abundance strengthens the assumption (zann) and thus gives preference. The text first presents the arguments of the Imamayn: (1) analogy to testimony (shahada): just as two witnesses are opposed by two, and four are opposed by four, without preference, so multiple proofs are opposed by equal multiplicity and all fall; (2) consensus (ijma') on inheritance cases: a cousin who is also a maternal brother is not preferred over a pure cousin, because each kinship is independent; similarly, a cousin who is also a husband does not block a pure cousin. These show that multiplicity of relations does not give extra strength. The author then criticizes these arguments: the first is weak because the whole together does give strength (like Zayd resists individuals but not the whole). The second and third are only valid if each relation individually gives 'usuba, but the husband and maternal brother do not. The author then explains that the gist of the first argument is that each proof is sufficient for the result, so combination adds nothing; this is why in debate one objection cannot be answered by another objection. The author notes that for certainties (qat'iyyat), abundance does not add strength, but for narrators, there is room for doubt and the social structure of tawatur does give strength. The majority's own argument is that zann is gradually strengthened by multiplicity, as in tawatur, so multiplicity gives tarjih. The author refutes this by pointing out that Umar and others did not prefer the hadith 'Innama al-ma'u al-ma'u' despite many narrators, but gave preference to the single report of Aisha. Finally, a counter-argument is raised: both schools agree that abundance of ijtihad does not give preference, yet it reaches certainty by consensus, similar to tawatur. The answer is that the certainty from consensus is devotional (ta'abbudi) and comes suddenly, not gradually, unlike the gradual strengthening of assumption from multiple narrators.

Key Terms
ترجيح (tarjih): Preference given to one of two conflicting evidences based on a preponderating factor.
كثرة الأدلة (kathrat al-adilla): Abundance of proofs or evidences on one side of a conflict.
شهرة (shuhra): Fame; a level of transmission below tawatur but above ahad, which may give added certainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Imamayn, why does multiplicity of proofs not give preference?
A) Because each proof is independent and sufficient for the result, so combination adds no strength.
B) Because the Quran prohibits considering multiplicity.
C) Because the majority consensus opposes it.
D) Because the Hadith of 'Innama al-ma'u al-ma'u' proves it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position is that mere abundance of independent proofs or narrators does not give tarjih; only when multiplicity reaches a level of social structure (like tawatur) does it add strength.

Al-Ijma — Consensus
advanced
10 min
Critique and Rebuttal of the Majority's Argument on Tarjih by Multiplicity
Critique of the Imamayn's arguments and rebuttal of the majority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the majority's argument for tarjih by multiplicity and explain the Hanafi rebuttal including the distinction between proofs and narrators.

Core Idea
The majority's gradual-strengthening argument applies only to narrators with social structure, not to independent proofs.

In the second part of the passage, the author critiques the arguments of the Imamayn themselves and then presents the majority's position that zann is gradually strengthened by multiplicity. The author then refutes this by pointing to the practice of the Companions (Umar preferring a single report) and by drawing a crucial distinction: gradual strengthening applies only to narrators where a social structure can emerge, not to independent proofs. The passage also addresses a counter-argument about ijtihad and consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's critique of the Imamayn's arguments
The author says the first argument (testimony analogy) is weak because the whole together does give strength (like Zayd resists individuals but not the whole). The second and third arguments only work if each kinship relation individually gives 'usuba (which husband and maternal brother do not). Recognize this as a critical refinement within the Hanafi tradition.
This shows internal Hanafi dialectic: the author is not blindly accepting all arguments but refining them.
2
Understand the author's defense of the first argument
Despite the critique, the author ultimately defends the core: each proof is sufficient for the result, so combination adds nothing. The gist is that in debate, one objection cannot be countered by another objection.
The author sides with the Imamayn's conclusion, not necessarily their original formulation.
3
Examine the majority's argument
The majority claims that zann strengthens gradually by multiplicity, reaching certainty in tawatur. Thus multiplicity gives tarjih. Identify the basis: analogy to tawatur where number leads to certainty.
This is the main opposing argument.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal using the Companion practice
The author cites the hadith 'Innama al-ma'u al-ma'u' which had many narrators, yet Umar and others preferred the single report of Aisha. This shows the Companions did not consider multiplicity of narrators as automatically giving preference.
This is a strong historical evidence against the majority.
5
Understand the counter-argument and its answer
A counter-argument is raised: both schools agree that abundance of ijtihad does not give tarjih, yet it reaches certainty by consensus. The author answers that the certainty from consensus is devotional (ta'abbudi) and comes suddenly, not by gradual strengthening of zann, unlike narrators.
This refines the distinction between gradual strengthening (narrators) and sudden devotional certainty (consensus).

For them, each proof is independent, so one proof opposes another, and another also opposes, so all fall when opposed, so there is no basis for preference, like testimony: just as the testimony of two opposes the testimony of two others, so also the testimony of four opposes. And for them also is consensus of all except Ibn Mas'ud among the Companions and those after them on not preferring a cousin who is also a maternal brother over one who is only a cousin in inheritance, so the former does not block the latter, but each kinship gives entitlement independently, similarly the many proofs each of which is a cause of knowledge are not preferred over one. And for them also is consensus of all on its absence, i.e., absence of preference, in the case of a cousin who is also a husband over only a cousin, despite the existence of two relationships, so likewise here. Yes, if there is abundance that has a social structure that necessitates something that individual parts do not independently, and the benefit of the individuals is conditioned by this structure, it gives additional strength undoubtedly, as in the mutawatir and mashhur. And it is not hidden from the intelligent the weakness of these arguments. As for the weakness of the first, the whole together gives strength of substantiation; do you not see that Zayd resists every individual but does not resist the whole? As for the second and third, they are only valid if each of the two kinship relations necessitates 'usuba, but that is not so, for the husband relation alone necessitates entitlement to half only, and maternal brotherhood alone necessitates entitlement to a sixth, as in al-Hashiya. And it does not escape you that the gist of the first argument is that each proof is entailing the result; in giving the result, each is sufficient, and just as it obtains from one proof, so from two, so no additional strength is obtained from combination, so the prevention is cut off. Hence it is heard in debate that the answer to one objection is not valid by another objection. And consider from yourself: if combination of proofs necessitated additional strength, then the abundance of proofs for certainties would have no meaning, for they do not accept strength and weakness; otherwise certainties would not remain sound. Yes, with abundance of narrators there is room for doubt, for the occasional social structure gives strength that was not there before, so understand. The gist of the second and third is that the kinship of 'usuba and husband or maternal brotherhood each is entailing entitlement to inheritance even if each is not 'usuba, and their combination gives no additional strength, so likewise the proofs, each being independently beneficial for the result, their combination gives no additional strength, so understand. And for the majority that assumption is gradually strengthened by abundance of informants until it reaches certainty by tawatur, so multiplicity gives strength, so preference is given. And it does not escape you that this does not apply to abundance of proofs, for gradual strengthening in it is denied, so the proof falls short of the claim. Then the narrators of 'Innama al-ma'u al-ma'u' were many, and Amir al-Mu'minin 'Umar and other Companions gave preference to the single report of the Mother of the Believers, and they did not consider strengthening by abundance of narrators; perhaps this strength is weak and its levels vary, so considering it involves some difficulty, and Allah desires ease. The answer is that the certainty obtained by consensus is devotional, obtained suddenly, not by gradual strengthening of the assumption from ijtihad by addition until it reaches certainty by consensus, unlike narrators, for assumption in them strengthens by addition and reaches certainty by tawatur, so understand.

Commentary
This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut discusses a dispute in usul al-fiqh regarding whether abundance of proofs (kathrat al-adilla) or narrators (kathrat al-ruwat) gives additional strength for tarjih (preference) when conflicting evidences are present. The issue is: if one has multiple proofs on one side and a single proof on the other, does the multiplicity give added weight? The Hanafi position, according to Imam al-A'zam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf (the Imamayn), is that there is no preference by mere multiplicity unless the evidence reaches the level of shuhra (fame). The majority (including Imam Muhammad, al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad) hold that abundance strengthens the assumption (zann) and thus gives preference. The text first presents the arguments of the Imamayn: (1) analogy to testimony (shahada): just as two witnesses are opposed by two, and four are opposed by four, without preference, so multiple proofs are opposed by equal multiplicity and all fall; (2) consensus (ijma') on inheritance cases: a cousin who is also a maternal brother is not preferred over a pure cousin, because each kinship is independent; similarly, a cousin who is also a husband does not block a pure cousin. These show that multiplicity of relations does not give extra strength. The author then criticizes these arguments: the first is weak because the whole together does give strength (like Zayd resists individuals but not the whole). The second and third are only valid if each relation individually gives 'usuba, but the husband and maternal brother do not. The author then explains that the gist of the first argument is that each proof is sufficient for the result, so combination adds nothing; this is why in debate one objection cannot be answered by another objection. The author notes that for certainties (qat'iyyat), abundance does not add strength, but for narrators, there is room for doubt and the social structure of tawatur does give strength. The majority's own argument is that zann is gradually strengthened by multiplicity, as in tawatur, so multiplicity gives tarjih. The author refutes this by pointing out that Umar and others did not prefer the hadith 'Innama al-ma'u al-ma'u' despite many narrators, but gave preference to the single report of Aisha. Finally, a counter-argument is raised: both schools agree that abundance of ijtihad does not give preference, yet it reaches certainty by consensus, similar to tawatur. The answer is that the certainty from consensus is devotional (ta'abbudi) and comes suddenly, not gradually, unlike the gradual strengthening of assumption from multiple narrators.

Key Terms
الهيئة الاجتماعية (al-hay'a al-ijtima'iyya): Social structure; the collective configuration of narrators that gives added certainty beyond the sum of individuals.
تعبدي (ta'abbudi): Devotional; a ruling or certainty that is derived from worship rather than rational reasoning, coming suddenly as a divine gift.
ظن (zann): Conjecture or probable opinion; a degree of certainty below yaqin (certainty).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rebuttal, why did Umar not prefer the hadith 'Innama al-ma'u al-ma'u' despite having many narrators?
A) Because the hadith was weak in chain.
B) Because the many narrators did not form a social structure that gives added strength.
C) Because Umar was following his own opinion.
D) Because the hadith contradicted the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rebuttal rests on three pillars: independence of proofs, historical practice of Companions, and the distinction between gradual strengthening (narrators with social structure) and sudden devotional certainty (consensus).

Defining Ijma: Linguistic and Terminological Foundations
Defining ijma and addressing objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and terminological definitions of ijma, identify the key conditions in its definition, and evaluate the objections regarding its possibility and knowledge.

Core Idea
Ijma is the agreement of the mujtahids of the ummah in an era on a religious ruling — a definitive proof despite objections.

This passage introduces ijma as the third source of Islamic law. It begins with the linguistic roots of the term, moves to the technical definition preferred by Hanafi usul, and then addresses two major objections: one about the impossibility of future consensus, and another about the impossibility of knowing and transmitting consensus. Understanding these foundational definitions is essential before exploring further disputes about the authority of ijma.

Guided Path
1
Parse the linguistic meaning of ijma
Identify the two linguistic meanings (azm and ittifaq) and their derivation from jamm (gathering). The first is exemplified by Quran 10:71 and the hadith about fasting.
Both meanings involve a form of gathering: determination gathers thoughts; agreement gathers opinions.
2
Understand the technical definition
The technical definition is agreement of all mujtahids of the ummah in one era on a religious ruling. Some scholars add conditions like extinction of the era or exclusion of preceding disagreement.
The author considers the definition without these conditions as correct for the concept; the conditions relate to authority.
3
Examine objections to al-Ghazali's definition
Two objections are raised: (1) future agreement is unknown, (2) non-universality if no mujtahid exists. The author shows only the second is valid and answers it by noting that an era without any mujtahid is contrary to reality.
Both objections can be avoided by specifying 'mujtahids in an era'.
4
Analyze the dispute about possibility of ijma
Some Nazzamiyya and Shia argued that ijma is impossible due to dispersion of scholars. The Hanafi response uses tawatur transmission (like the Quran) and the fact that early mujtahids were few and known.
Even al-Nazzam himself reportedly held ijma is conceivable but not a proof.
5
Consider epistemological challenges
Even if possible, knowing and transmitting consensus might be impossible. The response: tawatur solves knowledge; early concentration solves transmission; diligent scholars can find consensus.
If consensus occurs, it must be about a certain or probable matter; the text suggests further reasoning.

The third principle: Ijma (consensus). Linguistically: it means determination, as in the saying of Allah: 'So resolve upon your plan' [Yunus 71], and in his saying, peace be upon him: 'No fasting for one who does not determine to fast from the night.' (And agreement; both meanings) – i.e., that which means determination and that which means agreement – are derived from 'jamm' (gathering), for determination involves gathering thoughts and opinions. Terminologically: agreement of the mujtahids of this merciful and honored ummah (in an era) on a religious matter. Those who condition the extinction of the era add 'agreement continuing until extinction,' and those who consider the preceding difference add accordingly. The correct definition is this. The one who conditions one of the two matters only conditions for authority, so understand. Imam al-Ghazali (may Allah sanctify his soul) said: Ijma is the agreement of the ummah of Muhammad (peace be upon him and his family and companions) on a religious matter. It was objected, as in al-Mukhtasar, that it is inconceivable because agreement of future generations is not known, and that it is not universal if there is no mujtahid among them, and an agreement occurs from others besides the mujtahid, for it would be called agreement of the ummah but is not ijma. I say: The existing members of the ummah are either the ummah or not. On the first, the agreement of the ummah – and they are the existing ones – is certainly conceivable, and no consideration is given to those who are not. On the second, there is no room for the objection. So only one of the two objections applies, not both. The correct view is that the second applies because the apparent meaning of 'ummah' is the existing ones. The answer to it is that the matter of objection must be realized, and here it is denied, for the absence of a mujtahid in every era is contrary to reality. Both objections may be refuted by intending the agreement of the mujtahids in an era, because it is the apparent meaning in this context, as in his saying: 'My ummah will not agree upon an error.' He chose this figurative expression to achieve good citation, so understand. Some of the Nazzamiyya and Shia said that it is impossible, and it is attributed by more than one to al-Nazzam. al-Subki said: He only said it from some of his followers. As for al-Nazzam's own view, it is that it is conceivable but not a proof. This was narrated by Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi and Imam al-Razi. Thus in the gloss. Even if it is not impossible, knowledge of it is impossible; and even if knowledge is possible, its transmission to us is impossible. As for the first: occurrence, firstly because their dispersion in regions prevents the transmission of the ruling to them normally, and if transmission of the ruling is prevented, their agreement is prevented. The answer: This is mere claim; there is no prevention in mutawatir like the Quran, for due to its fame it is not hidden from anyone; and no prevention in early Islam either, because the mujtahid imams were few and known, so transmission of the ruling to them was facilitated; and no prevention after their diligence in seeking and researching, for what is sought is not hidden from the diligent seeker. Secondly, if it occurs, then it is either about a certain matter or a probable one, and if it were...

Commentary
This passage defines ijma (consensus) as a source of Islamic law. It gives the linguistic meaning: determination (azm) and agreement (ittifaq), both derived from 'jamm' (gathering). The technical definition is: agreement of the mujtahids of the ummah in a given era on a religious ruling. Some scholars add conditions like the extinction of the era or exclude previous disagreement. The correct definition does not require these conditions for authority; rather, they are conditions for the authority of ijma. The text then discusses al-Ghazali's definition and two objections: (1) impossibility because future agreement is unknown, and (2) non-universality if there is no mujtahid. The author argues that only the second objection is valid because 'ummah' ordinarily means the existing ones, and the answer is that an era without any mujtahid is contrary to reality. Both objections can be avoided by explicitly stating 'mujtahids in an era.' Next, the text addresses the view of some Nazzamiyya and Shia that ijma is impossible, and al-Nazzam's actual position that it is conceivable but not a proof. Finally, it considers the impossibility of knowing ijma and transmitting it. The answer is that transmission is possible via tawatur, as with the Quran, and in early Islam the mujtahids were few and known, making transmission easy.

Key Terms
الإجماع (al-ijmā‘): Agreement of the mujtahids of the ummah in a given era on a religious ruling.
العزم (al-‘azm): Determination or resolve; one of the linguistic meanings of ijma.
الاتفاق (al-ittifāq): Agreement; the other linguistic meaning of ijma.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It is required only in matters of worship

Key Takeaway
Ijma is the consensus of mujtahids of an era on a religious ruling; despite objections about possibility and knowledge, it is a valid and authoritative source through tawatur and early scholarly concentration.
